  <a href="#other_reviews" class="actionLinkLite votes">
      <span itemprop="ratingCount" title="831" class="value-title">831 Ratings</span>
</a>  <span class="greyText"> · </span>
  <a href="#other_reviews" class="actionLinkLite">
      <span class="count"><span title="102" class="value-title">102</span></span>
      Reviews

<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12421599066148053380">Walter Mosley, the author of the Easy Rawlins mystery novels (Devil in a Blue Dress; Black Betty; etc.) has a new story to tell. Instead of coasting on his success (Bill Clinton is a fan), in 1998 he took a left turn and published Blue Light, an allegorical sort-of science fiction novel that never quite found its voice, although it was a New York Times Notable Book. Blue L</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12421599066148053380">Walter Mosley, the author of the Easy Rawlins mystery novels (Devil in a Blue Dress; Black Betty; etc.) has a new story to tell. Instead of coasting on his success (Bill Clinton is a fan), in 1998 he took a left turn and published Blue Light, an allegorical sort-of science fiction novel that never quite found its voice, although it was a New York Times Notable Book. Blue Light must have been Mosley's SF false start, because Futureland rocks and roars from the first page. <br /><br />Ostensibly a collection of short stories, Futureland reads more like a William Gibson novel. Minor characters in one story are major characters in another story, which gives the reader that MTV jump-cut sensation, but not so frantic with Mosley as it is with Gibson. <br /><br />And what characters! The charm of Mosley's earlier books is that the characters are normal people in historical settings, getting by as best they can. But in Futureland the reader encounters the heavyweight boxing champion of the world (female); the most intelligent human ever; the mad scientist at the head of the world's largest corporation. These characters are neither normal nor historical. <br /><br />So with sly nods to Hegel and DeLillo, Mosley abandons history and the common man. It's a great privilege for a reader to watch a good writer change his mind. In an essay on his website Mosley claims to have been an SF fan all his life. He knows the genre; he knows the language (before he published his first novel he was a computer programmer); and he knows that there is no better venue to change your mind than SF. <br /><br />Mosley retains some of his favorite themes, though. All the exceptional characters in the book are full members of lively communities. The best story in the book-no surprise-is about a private eye. Like most hardboiled detectives, Folio Johnson needs friends on the force and on the street to survive. Unlike Easy Rawlins, though, he also needs wetware implants to keep from going down. <br /><br />And race pervades this book like it pervades all Mosley's books. It is a theme handled well in all of the stories except for the final one ("The Nig in Me," go figure), where it is too over-the-top, and detracts from a lightweight story that was an SF cliché in the first place. <br /><br />Also in the essay on his website, Mosley asserts that the next five years will see a wave of black SF writers. If Mosley stays with this genre, he'll lead them. He's as good as anyone writing SF today.<br />_ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _<br /><br />Futureland is bestselling mystery author Walter Mosley's first science fiction book since Blue Light, a New York Times Notable Book of the Year. Futureland's nine linked stories will provide an accessible and intelligent introduction to written science fiction for mystery or mainstream fiction fans who do not normally read the genre. Experienced science fiction readers, however, may be less than satisfied with Futureland. Reading it, you might decide Mr. Mosley grew up reading SF, respects the genre, and still watches SF movies, but has read little SF written during or after the New Wave of the 1960s. However, something more may be going on here than a genre newcomer making beginning-SF-writer mistakes. Mr. Mosley may be deliberately, and craftily, creating SF accessible to his large non-SF readership and to others who are strangers to this genre. Some have labeled Futureland cyberpunk, and it does present a dark, infotech-saturated, corporation-controlled future; but it is in fact an inversion of cyberpunk. Instead of that subgenre's cliche of cool, cutting-edge, street-smart, but not very believable outlaws who out-hack and outwit powerful multinational corporations, this Dante-esque collection presents outlaws and outcasts who may be street-wise, but who have little chance of overcoming the corporations and governments that control, and sometimes take, their lives. Like shockingly few other SF works, Futureland directly examines the lives of the working and the nonworking classes, the poor and the marginalized, the criminal and the criminalized. In other words, Futureland is set in a world quite alien to many veteran SF readers, and is therefore a book they should try. --Cynthia Ward</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12421599066148053380">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_379574085">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/379574085" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5457013-eli" class="left imgcol" title="Eli"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1433737794p2/5457013.jpg" alt="Eli" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/379574085?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 27, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5457013-eli" class="user" title="Eli">Eli</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5457013-eli?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5457013-eli?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5457013-eli?shelf=utopia-dystopia" class="actionLinkLite">utopia-dystopia</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5457013-eli?shelf=collections-anthologies" class="actionLinkLite">collections-anthologies</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5457013-eli?shelf=alternate-history" class="actionLinkLite">alternate-history</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5457013-eli?shelf=cyberpunk" class="actionLinkLite">cyberpunk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer379574085">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9287595010575148002">This anthology has created a future that is both advanced and backward –– a world where technology has played a drastic role in both large-scale convenience and oppression. In this world, we see how such technology has been used in different ways: how it is a tool that can expand, or limit, the breadths of human horizon. This is very evident in the way people are segregated. Those who belong to the upper classes stay high above the ground, and those who own nothing stay deep underneath… literall</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9287595010575148002">This anthology has created a future that is both advanced and backward –– a world where technology has played a drastic role in both large-scale convenience and oppression. In this world, we see how such technology has been used in different ways: how it is a tool that can expand, or limit, the breadths of human horizon. This is very evident in the way people are segregated. Those who belong to the upper classes stay high above the ground, and those who own nothing stay deep underneath… literally.<br /><br />There are 9 stories in this anthology, and I can’t pick which one I like best. The stories I really liked were: <em>Whispers in the Dark</em>, <em>The Greatest</em>, <em>Angel’s Island</em>, and <em>The Electric Eye</em>. The timeline is not explicitly stated, but it can be inferred that the stories are in chronological order. The stories are not directly connected with each other, but we can think that each of them is a torn part of a single photograph –– the bigger picture that shows a system that is highly organized and yet broken in a lot of aspects.<br /><br />To my delight, the characters were well-developed despite the limited number of pages. It is basically the same world for every story, but every character offers a different take on it. It was interesting to read how all their lives are linked in a subtle manner to represent the different forms of devastation that one can experience in that society. The writing style was pleasant as it sets the motion of the stories in the right pace.<br /><br />This is the first work I’ve read from this author and it was a good experience. However, one should be warned that a happy tale is not to be expected, but I don’t think this will rob any reader of a good time. <em>Futureland</em> is a fine read that draws haunting images of a future we should be scared of. It’s a nice blend of science fiction, mystery, and dark humor that is gripping and thought-provoking.<br /><br />4.5 stars.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9287595010575148002">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1323681813">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1323681813" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4222001-kathy-davie" class="left imgcol" title="Kathy Davie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1285089902p2/4222001.jpg" alt="Kathy Davie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1323681813?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4222001-kathy-davie" class="user" title="Kathy Davie">Kathy Davie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18894340-futureland" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1323681813">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1264826749022830818">Nine short stories of horrifying science fiction, of a future that simply continues the problems of today with more advanced science. Each story provides a different look at this future society, and it isn't nice. It isn't a world in which I want to live, where your birth or lack of employment condemns you to a life of nothing. Where the system is sucking out man's ingenuity and spirit for the sake of production and profit.<br /><br />The stories tie loosely together with a few common characters that crop u</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1264826749022830818">Nine short stories of horrifying science fiction, of a future that simply continues the problems of today with more advanced science. Each story provides a different look at this future society, and it isn't nice. It isn't a world in which I want to live, where your birth or lack of employment condemns you to a life of nothing. Where the system is sucking out man's ingenuity and spirit for the sake of production and profit.<br /><br />The stories tie loosely together with a few common characters that crop up here and there. There's Akwande and his idealism and Tristan the First, Dominar of the Blue Zone, and his shared lack of compassion or humanity with Kismet. Folio Johnson is a far-seeing man — no pun intended — who has a sense of justice. Fera Jones, Pell, and Professor Jones have their ups and downs. And D'or of the China Diner who is one of the last remnants of our past is a common thread.<br /><br />This ARC was sent to me by NetGalley and Open Road Media for an honest review.<br /><br /><strong>The Stories</strong><br />"<strong>Whispers in the Dark</strong>" will make you cry as Chilly Bent does his best to take care of his dying mother and brilliant young nephew. It's a future where the government takes your child if he or she tests smart. It's a future where interfering old biddies still think they know better than you. And it's an ending that makes me want to know more. Lots more.<br /><br />"<strong>The Greatest</strong>" finds Fera Jones, a woman boxer, up against Jellyroll Gregory, and she's doing well. Beating men in KO fights. But she can't lose one lest her father lose access to the Pulse. It's a story of feminine rights, of standing up for yourself, of turning it around on the rich.<br /><br />The flip and uncaring "<strong>Doctor Kismet</strong>" has his own island kingdom, Home, and owns most of the world, both business-wise and in religion. A benevolent tyrant as long as he wins, it's a contest of good versus evil, of idealism versus reality with a reference to Popo from "Whispers in the Dark". It's a dream Akwande has of relieving death and suffering in Africa. One he struggles to achieve only to realize that truth, that reality.<br /><br />"<strong>Angel's Island</strong>" is a futuristic prison that sounds like a nightmare. One that only gets worse. I gotta say that I'm in favor of capital punishment, but I am not in favor of lying. Nor am I in favor of what is done in this prison. The <em>whys</em> of it. It's just nasty. And I gotta wonder about the justice of such a system. Their ideas of poor behavior.<br /><br />"<strong>The Electric Eye</strong>" is both terrifying and exciting. I love the idea of Folio Johnson's eye, although how he got it is not my idea of a good time. And that's not the terrifying part. That comes at the end when Folio learns the horrid truth behind those assassinations.<br /><br />"<strong>Voices</strong>" finds us dipping back into Fera Jones' life, as we meet back up with her Pulse-addicted father, Professor Leon Jones, and the treatment he's undergoing to save his life.<br /><blockquote>"I think we're all the same color, just more of some colors and not so many of others."</blockquote>It's another terrifying story of future possibilities. One that entices you in, gives you hope that future medical care truly can save you. And then it makes you want to run in fear, hoping, hoping to be saved from something more invasive, more threatening. I couldn't actually tell what happened at the end, as Mosley leaves it rather vague.<br /><br />"<strong>Little Brother</strong>" explores the future's judicial system, and it hasn't changed from being a system that caters to the rich. What has changed is its automatic "capability" and a deeper lesson in why allowing man to set up a judicial system is a bad idea. It's another ending in which Mosley leaves us wondering. <br />"<strong>En Masse</strong>" is a nightmare <em>1984</em> as it looks at the mechanics and requirements of employment in the future. Anything can be an infraction and anything can get you fired. Being fired sends you down to Common Ground and the horrifying possibility of never working again.<br /><br />Oh, lol, "En Masse" takes "in the cloud" to a whole new meaning. A system that wants to return man to being an innovative, thinking person with spirit. It's another depressing ending as we don't know the results of one half of the mission, and Neil…well, Neil isn't in much shape for anything.<br /><br />"<strong>The Nig in Me</strong>" is all that's keeping Harold alive. It is gawd-awful depressing, but made me laugh about those "Caucasians" who discovered something about their genetic heritage. Yep, it's a brave new world after the virus from "En Masse" got changed around. Only, it's not brave or new. It's the same ol', same ol'.<br /><br /><strong>The Cover and Title</strong><br />A slim band of red frames in the cover at top and bottom in the yellow-green lights of the towering highrises against a misty gray night. The author's name and the title are in white using a digital font, a reflection of this <em>Futureland</em> of fear.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1264826749022830818">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1049199056">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1049199056" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13776299-vincent" class="left imgcol" title="Vincent"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1350668172p2/13776299.jpg" alt="Vincent" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1049199056?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 08, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13776299-vincent" class="user" title="Vincent">Vincent</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1049199056">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11574990702747941065">I picked up this e-book from our local library, knowing nothing about the author. I just read the description on the library's website and thought, as a casual sci-fi fan, it sounded intruiging. Wow. I'm totally blown away.<br /><br />This is a story (actually several stories that interconnect) about a frighteningly possible world. Corporate greed, social immobility, and wealth-inequity taken to logical extremes in an all-too-near future. A world far closer in some regards than when it was written (2001).<br /><br />Mo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11574990702747941065">I picked up this e-book from our local library, knowing nothing about the author. I just read the description on the library's website and thought, as a casual sci-fi fan, it sounded intruiging. Wow. I'm totally blown away.<br /><br />This is a story (actually several stories that interconnect) about a frighteningly possible world. Corporate greed, social immobility, and wealth-inequity taken to logical extremes in an all-too-near future. A world far closer in some regards than when it was written (2001).<br /><br />Mosley allows us to slowly crawl into the world he's created. The first story starts in the rural Southern US. One of the characters speaks (and is therefore written) with a speech impediment from a stroke; others speak with heavy accents. Be patient. As we move from story to story, from location to location and from character to character, a world starts to form around you. This is a dystopia, but even more-so a chess game, seen from the point of view of the pawns. Perhaps even a 3D chess game, where the strata are the levels of a future New York City in which upper/mid/lower refer not to latitudes but altitudes. The poorer classes live "lower", closer to a ground level nearly devoid of sunlight. The completely disenfranchised live lower still, in the Common Ground where citizenship and life itself are marginalized.<br /><br />Each of the earlier stories is independent (a love story, a mystery, etc.) with a few characters that are discussed or pass through from one to another. The stories work together, though, in creating the frame that houses the final two stories that are simultaneously personal and global.<br /><br />I could go on and on about the clever ways Mosley constructs the social mores of this world and how he introduces them to the reader. I won't, though. Far more interesting for you to discover them for yourself.<br /><br />Side note: this was excellent on the Kindle because I was able to use X-ray to easily find where characters popped up earlier in the book so I could reintroduce myself to them.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11574990702747941065">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_41895665">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41895665" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1190411-wizzard" class="left imgcol" title="Wizzard"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1213406669p2/1190411.jpg" alt="Wizzard" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/41895665?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 13, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1190411-wizzard" class="user" title="Wizzard">Wizzard</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1190411-wizzard?shelf=black-culture-history-and-society" class="actionLinkLite">black-culture-history-and-society</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41895665">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4497377187754948752">I realized from my readings over the Christmas season why I enjoy well-written books from Black culture more than most science fiction books (though I do like them a great deal!). They rank higher in my favorite books because these treasures ring true to what I know, they convey in their narratives truths about Black culture and its complex situatedness to America. Science fiction often catapults from reality to a clean slate largely of the author’s imagination. The stories in Mosley’s Futurelan</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4497377187754948752">I realized from my readings over the Christmas season why I enjoy well-written books from Black culture more than most science fiction books (though I do like them a great deal!). They rank higher in my favorite books because these treasures ring true to what I know, they convey in their narratives truths about Black culture and its complex situatedness to America. Science fiction often catapults from reality to a clean slate largely of the author’s imagination. The stories in Mosley’s Futureland combine strengths of both aspects. The stories are rich in cultural observance and Black stylings while the force of Mosley’s imagination is in full effect in this complex corporate world of the future.<br /><br />I like the book because Mosley really plays with culture. Sometimes he takes familiar elements –even verging on stereotypes—and jazzes them up. Other times he jams together disparate parts and conflicting ideas. Throughout the book, characters enter, leave, and re-enter to create a powerful Futureland world populated by detectives and criminals, athletes and their fans, sex workers production workers and genius tycoons,.<br /><br />“The Greatest” is one of the stories I truly enjoyed reading. It’s a sports story, and a love story. It accelerates into social commentary when the protagonist, a female boxing champion considers her power outside the ring. The story takes the Tiger Woods’ and Venus Williams’ celebrity and elaborates it for an exaggerated future and then draws it outside of the world of sports to raise a critical lens to our relationships with athletics and celebrity.<br /><br />Still, I didn’t like every story evenly. I liked reading them all but I didn’t feel the same urgency of reading throughout the whole book. A few stories I read just to see how the story His sillyness and cultural symbolism reminds me of Ishmael Reed. A great sci-fi find.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4497377187754948752">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_80585220">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80585220" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3030737-flying-monkey" class="left imgcol" title="Flying_Monkey"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Flying_Monkey" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/80585220?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 10, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3030737-flying-monkey" class="user" title="Flying_Monkey">Flying_Monkey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer80585220">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7014267856587551247">Having though 'Blue Light' one of the most fantastic new SF novels of recent years, and having been amazed at its poor reception from Mosley fans, I was delighted and surprised to see him back with another work of SF, and one which deals with many of the same themes as his previous genre work, but in markedly different ways.<br /><br />The worst thing about 'Futureland' is its title - I suspect an editor wary of making the book too inaccessible to non-SF readers (or perhaps even Mosley himself worrying abou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7014267856587551247">Having though 'Blue Light' one of the most fantastic new SF novels of recent years, and having been amazed at its poor reception from Mosley fans, I was delighted and surprised to see him back with another work of SF, and one which deals with many of the same themes as his previous genre work, but in markedly different ways.<br /><br />The worst thing about 'Futureland' is its title - I suspect an editor wary of making the book too inaccessible to non-SF readers (or perhaps even Mosley himself worrying about such problems), but whoever made the choice, it does not exactly sparkle with originality or invite wonder in anyone approaching the book. It deserved better.<br /><br />The book itself is composed of nine linked stories set in a world where corporations have divided up the planet, and people are forced to live according to strict socio-economic and geographic constraints, even to the extent that New York, for example, is divided into three horizontal layers, where the poorest never see the sunlight. America remains the dominant power but it is forced to export many of its social problems: the growing prison population is now housed on privately-run islands where the drug-controlled prison population is used as slave labour. And of course, those who bear the brunt of this crushingly divided world order are black. Race and gender politics are everywhere in this book, from the new opportunities generated by a world champion boxer who is a black woman (clearly drawn as a female Muhammed Ali) who can beat the best male fighters, to the onward march of the International Socialists, a depressingly realistic neo-Nazi movement. The latter are dismissed by various characters in the book as unimportant, marginal or simply 'conservative' (a justification used by a member), however they gradually assume a central importance as the trajectories woven in the various separate tales are threaded together towards the final stories. As in Blue Light, the tone of the conclusion is downbeat, the final story almost an epigraph, despite the overt hope of renewal.<br /><br />As an SF setting, and even as a collection of short stories, 'Futureland' might not stand up to close examination were it not for three factors. The first is Mosley's righteously angry politics (mentioned above), the second is his obvious love for the genre, and his knowledge of its past. One review I read compared 'Futureland' very unfavourably to the work of William Gibson, as cyberpunk fiction. However I feel this misses the point. Gibson also understands the context in which SF is written, witness his fabulous early story, The Gernsback Continuum, which mixes Twilight Zone style plotting and the 'airships and Aryans-in-togas' imagery from the 1930s pulp magazines, yet which makes a very contemporary point about memory and its relationship to our visions of the future. Mosley also mixes all sorts of iconic SF images into his work: there are the info-monks, with their blue cloaks and their brains made visible by plastic domes, there is a super-intelligent megalomaniac attempting to rebuild Atlantis and colonize Mars, and an equally gifted child prodigy who finds ways of speaking to God through radio noise. There are also SF images from the New Wave period: a world-weary 'electronic private eye', a man suddenly startled to find his dull existence turned downside-up by a fortune he struggles to understand, a prisoner who can liberate himself and others only through his own death and so on. The final factor is Mosley's ironic sensibility. These iconic SF devices are skillfully strung together with (also like Gibson) a delightful and sometimes disturbing use of irony: for example, Vietnam which has struggled to liberate itself from the French and then the Americans, and then (some might argue) from its own form of communism, has succeeded, only to find itself divided up and owned by trans-national corporations.<br /><br />'Futureland' doesn't succeed entirely, and this is largely dues to the variable quality of the stories. Some, like the opener Whispers in the Dark; the prison drama, Angel's Island; the future private detective tale, The Electric Eye; and the multi-layered both hopeful and disturbing closer, The Nig in Me; are superb - others read more like fillers. Perhaps this is simply personal preference. The only work I can think of that compares to 'Futureland' is John Brunner's massive New Wave dystopia, Stand on Zanzibar, another ambitious brilliant-but-flawed work packed with irony, from an equally angry and socially-aware author. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7014267856587551247">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_942940124">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/942940124" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4054541-wayne-mccoy" class="left imgcol" title="Wayne McCoy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1331517656p2/4054541.jpg" alt="Wayne McCoy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/942940124?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 29, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4054541-wayne-mccoy" class="user" title="Wayne McCoy">Wayne McCoy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer942940124">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14986727149920333170">'Futureland' by Walter Mosley is a collection of nine Science Fiction stories he wrote a number of years ago collected into an ebook format. There is a distinct cyberpunk feel to the stories, and they intertwine some of the characters.<br /><br />We are introduced to a young boy named Ptolemy Bent whose father will go to great lengths to make sure his son gets the right education. We meet Fera Jones, a heavyweight fighter and her designer drug addicted father. Folio Jones is the "last private detective in N</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14986727149920333170">'Futureland' by Walter Mosley is a collection of nine Science Fiction stories he wrote a number of years ago collected into an ebook format. There is a distinct cyberpunk feel to the stories, and they intertwine some of the characters.<br /><br />We are introduced to a young boy named Ptolemy Bent whose father will go to great lengths to make sure his son gets the right education. We meet Fera Jones, a heavyweight fighter and her designer drug addicted father. Folio Jones is the "last private detective in New York" and has a computer for an eye.<br /><br />This is the future, but it's a future where rights and privileges are nonexistent for the common people. Rich, megomaniacal people rule the poor, run the prisons and collect the few who are smart enough to benefit them. Racism is still very much rampant, even to the extent of biowars. <br /><br />It's a bleak future, but the stories are interesting, the characters are memorable, and the message is loud and clear. This is great stuff from someone who isn't known for this type of writing.<br /><br />I was given a review copy of this ebook by Open Road Integrated Media and Netgalley in exchange for an honest review. Thank you for allowing me to review this book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14986727149920333170">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1068925199">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1068925199" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7072361-alberto" class="left imgcol" title="Alberto"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1335339715p2/7072361.jpg" alt="Alberto" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1068925199?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 01, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7072361-alberto" class="user" title="Alberto">Alberto</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1068925199">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18038075569788883322">I took a look at the first story from this 'Futureland' and thought "it's Ok but I'm not satisfied with its ending". Then I started reading the second one and I really liked it, so I went on with the third... and in few days I had read them all even though it is not such a thin book. I am far less proficient in English than in Spanish, but I was able to feel the colourful and suffocating atmosphere that Mosley chose for his near future world.<br />In short: I think this is quite a remarkable book, may</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18038075569788883322">I took a look at the first story from this 'Futureland' and thought "it's Ok but I'm not satisfied with its ending". Then I started reading the second one and I really liked it, so I went on with the third... and in few days I had read them all even though it is not such a thin book. I am far less proficient in English than in Spanish, but I was able to feel the colourful and suffocating atmosphere that Mosley chose for his near future world.<br />In short: I think this is quite a remarkable book, maybe not a SciFi masterpiece, but very well written, with interesting plots that converge and diverge from story to story picturing a sad and unfair society that, given the present circumstances, does not seem too unlikely. I enjoyed it very much. The only 'major' flaws I can point at are its analogies with other 'milestones' of the genre as Bradbury, Orwell, Huxley and even Cyberpunk subgenre -although it has its own (and strong) personality- and again the last story was less surprising and convincing than the rest of the book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18038075569788883322">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1131768372">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4146611205829975241">Extremely pleased with how much I ended up enjoying this fascinating set of stories. I don't recall how I heard about this one but I was thoroughly engrossed. I typically like the style of this type of collection, where stories build on one another, and this was done with more success and style than I remember encountering in a long time. The stories themselves are eerie, smart, scary, and deep. I will be seeking out more by Mosley.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a href="/review/list/6267491-victoria-law?shelf=dystopia" class="actionLinkLite">dystopia</a>,

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1098725839">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13576976118463971152">I don't usually enjoy short stories, but Mosley deftly weaves a frightening future where corporations have gigantic powers and people have next to none. It reminds me of <a title="Daniel José Older" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5137530.Daniel_Jos__Older">Daniel José Older</a>'s <a title="Salsa Nocturna by Daniel José Older" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21680226.Salsa_Nocturna">Salsa Nocturna</a> in that we follow several different and unconnected characters as they make their way through this horrifying dystopic future. There's a young genius (my favorite detail about him is that, at the age of four, he was trying to grow his hair to be like Albert Einstein's), a female boxer, a privat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13576976118463971152">I don't usually enjoy short stories, but Mosley deftly weaves a frightening future where corporations have gigantic powers and people have next to none. It reminds me of <a title="Daniel José Older" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5137530.Daniel_Jos__Older">Daniel José Older</a>'s <a title="Salsa Nocturna by Daniel José Older" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21680226.Salsa_Nocturna">Salsa Nocturna</a> in that we follow several different and unconnected characters as they make their way through this horrifying dystopic future. There's a young genius (my favorite detail about him is that, at the age of four, he was trying to grow his hair to be like Albert Einstein's), a female boxer, a private investigator with an eye that allows him to access information from anywhere on the 'net, a revolutionary and a man who instigates a mass breakout of the non-national privately run prison on Angel Island. </span>

  <a href="/user/show/1047102-paul" class="left imgcol" title="Paul"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Paul" /></a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_918097882">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/918097882" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/17324595-suzy-wilson" class="left imgcol" title="Suzy Wilson"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1361059056p2/17324595.jpg" alt="Suzy Wilson" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/918097882?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 30, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/17324595-suzy-wilson" class="user" title="Suzy Wilson">Suzy Wilson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer918097882">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11301013278885751966">Oh, I love me an anthology, particularly where you have a common basis for the tales. Walter Mosley's FUTURELAND is a collection of interrelated stories that take place in the same dystopic universe. In this world of capital consumerism gone wild, gargantuan corporations society. The elites live high in the sky, literally above everyone else. The unemployed love underground, condemned to live in purgatory, never starving, but never escaping the singularity of their existence. In between live eve</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11301013278885751966">Oh, I love me an anthology, particularly where you have a common basis for the tales. Walter Mosley's FUTURELAND is a collection of interrelated stories that take place in the same dystopic universe. In this world of capital consumerism gone wild, gargantuan corporations society. The elites live high in the sky, literally above everyone else. The unemployed love underground, condemned to live in purgatory, never starving, but never escaping the singularity of their existence. In between live everyone else - renting their lives from the corporations that control and sustain them, slaves to the State Owned Enterprises (or enterprise-owned State, I can't quite figure out which). <br /><br />The book is less of a novel, and more a series of vignettes, with overlapping characters and emerging, often tangled threads. The theme of race and racism is one of these commonalities. Some of the stories are more interdependent than others, but each introduces a range of compelling characters, who exist in a seemingly random chaos, with intersections of blinding, but fleeting connection. When read in sequence, each builds upon the other like a kaleidoscope - to give you fragments and glimpses of a threaded plot. Some characters recur and you start to get intimations of a book-long plot. <br /><br />Mosley's writing is strong and lyrical and it drags you along, sometimes resisting, but mostly compliant, towards the denouement ... But leaves you somewhat nonplussed at the journey. <br /><br />A fragmentary, yet oddly satisfactory foray into a complex and unsettling dystopia.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11301013278885751966">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_829022104">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/829022104" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6423134-bernie-gourley" class="left imgcol" title="Bernie Gourley"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1325610359p2/6423134.jpg" alt="Bernie Gourley" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/829022104?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 17, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6423134-bernie-gourley" class="user" title="Bernie Gourley">Bernie Gourley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer829022104">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8847030599138307786">As suggested by the subtitle, this is a collection of nine short stories about a dystopian world. What makes it a particularly intriguing read is that the stories take place in one world, and the events all exist within a greater context that could qualify the book as a loosely-plotted novel had the writer not defined it as a story collection. <br /><br />Some characters recur in different stories. For the most part the recurring characters are cameo appearances (e.g. Folio Johnson, a detective and the lead</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8847030599138307786">As suggested by the subtitle, this is a collection of nine short stories about a dystopian world. What makes it a particularly intriguing read is that the stories take place in one world, and the events all exist within a greater context that could qualify the book as a loosely-plotted novel had the writer not defined it as a story collection. <br /><br />Some characters recur in different stories. For the most part the recurring characters are cameo appearances (e.g. Folio Johnson, a detective and the lead in one story, commiserates at a bar in another). However, the character of Ptolemy “Popo” Bent is a critical character in both the first and penultimate chapters. <br /><br />Race and politics aren’t subtle in this book. Given the [sad] proclivity of American readers to only read / enjoy politically-charged works with which they agree (unless the book in question is making fun of the opposition), it’s safe to say that—on the whole--those at the left-end of the political spectrum will find this book more palatable and on-point and those to the right-end will find it unbelievable and overbearing in its message. <br /><br />Having said that, I’m of the persuasion that finds Mosley’s dystopian vision strains credulity, and yet I thoroughly enjoyed this collection of stories. This book’s dystopia is characterized by global domination by a corporation and a religion called the “Infochurch,” both led by the same man. The corporate control of the world storyline is a little hard to swallow. A monopoly can’t enslave people (or even enforce its monopoly status) unless it has a monopoly on force, and it’s hard to imagine a path by which a private business gets the people to give them a monopoly on force. That being said, Mosley’s stories are engrossing, creative, and readable. <br /><br />The nine stories are as follows:<br /><br />1.)	<em>Whispers in the Dark</em> (6 Chapters): A man makes the ultimate sacrifice to help nurture a brilliant child’s special gift.<br /><br />2.)	<em>The Greatest</em> (9 Chapters): A female boxer becomes the world champion while seeking to help her father, whose addition to a drug called Pulse has left him in dire health. (The father’s story, <em>Voices</em>, appears later in the collection.)<br /><br />3.)	<em>Dr. Kismet</em> (4 Chapters): The man who is, for all intents and purposes, Emperor of the World tries to coopt the co-chair of the 6th Radical Congress—a leading member of his opposition.<br /><br />4.)	<em>Angel’s Island</em> (5 Chapters): A hacker, sent to prison for Antisocial Behavior, has a device called a snake-pack installed that can control him by administration of drugs and shocks. But the ultimate hacker might not be the most easily controlled using technology.<br /><br />5.)	<em>Electric Eye</em> (4 Chapters): Folio Johnson, a private eye with an electronic eye, is hired to find out why young International Socialists are dropping dead left and right. Johnson learns that any hardware, even his eye, can be hacked.<br /><br />6.)	<em>Voices</em> (8 Chapters): Professor Jones, father of the female boxer from <em>The Greatest</em>, undergoes a transplant of neural matter to repair damage from his Pulse addition. After having dreams and memories that are not his own, Jones discovers that his treatment is not all that it seemed.<br /><br />7.)	<em>Little Brother</em> (3 Chapters): Frendon Blythe is on trial before a computer that acts as both judge and prosecutor. He pleads his own case, and finds he was a pawn. <br /><br />8.)	<em>En Masse</em> (12 Chapters): A worker gets sent to a new division only to find that it’s nothing like his previous divisions. Instead of strict rules, GEE-PRO-9 has no rules. He wonders if it might be a test by the management. It turns out that it is a test--just not of the type he imagined.<br /><br />9.)	<em>The Nig in Me</em> (6 Chapters): After a plot to destroy certain races backfires, a surviving man finds himself missing those with whom he was closest. <br /><br />There’re no stinkers among these stories. They are all intriguing and readable, but a few of them stood out as being particularly good. These were: <em>Whispers in the Dark</em>, <em>Angel’s Island</em>, <em>Voices</em>, and <em>En Masse</em>.<br /><br /><br />I’d recommend this for readers of soft science fiction. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8847030599138307786">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_75636130">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75636130" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2872252-kelly" class="left imgcol" title="Kelly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1256359222p2/2872252.jpg" alt="Kelly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/75636130?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 24, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2872252-kelly" class="user" title="Kelly">Kelly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer75636130">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5738225508639626392">I've read a lot of science fiction, much of it possibly by black writers. I don't know; I don't usually pay attention to the skin color of the author. Anyway, this is really the first concentrated dose of Black Science Fiction I've ever read. I feel as though I'm missing a significant part of the reading experience by being white, which is the main reason I didn't give this five stars. This is neither my fault nor Mosley's. No matter how much I empathize with a minority (in America) race, I cann</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5738225508639626392">I've read a lot of science fiction, much of it possibly by black writers. I don't know; I don't usually pay attention to the skin color of the author. Anyway, this is really the first concentrated dose of Black Science Fiction I've ever read. I feel as though I'm missing a significant part of the reading experience by being white, which is the main reason I didn't give this five stars. This is neither my fault nor Mosley's. No matter how much I empathize with a minority (in America) race, I cannot magically have grown up with the experiences of anyone belonging to one of those races. I cannot understand truly; I cannot feel it in my bones. Thus, I think four stars, for me, is my maximum level of enjoyment--again, nobody's fault.<br /><br />The stories are immensely enjoyable and very well written. I was especially and increasingly pleased at how the book starts as a series of unrelated characters and storylines, but then things start connecting in later stories. Mosley successfully weaves a beautiful tapestry this way--a dark, disturbing tapestry much of the time, but beautiful in its fullness and complexity.<br /><br />As I said, the biggest reason for the missing star is my non-blackness. The other contributing factor is my philosophical disagreement with what appears to an underlying pessimism in Mosley's view on race issues. Throughout the book, a current of despair flows unceasingly, sometimes a trickle, sometimes a raging torrent. Some stories do present and discuss very real possibilities for lasting change and reconciliation, but in the end the reader is left with a sense of hopelessness: lighter people and darker people will never get along and will always tend to separate themselves into opposing factions based upon appearances. I disagree with this, and that takes up the rest of the fifth star.<br /><br />Nonetheless, this is an excellent read for all fans of speculative fiction, no matter your race or color. It certainly does make one think, and isn't that the point?</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5738225508639626392">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_129948283">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129948283" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3977252-wayne" class="left imgcol" title="Wayne"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1350418019p2/3977252.jpg" alt="Wayne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/129948283?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 15, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3977252-wayne" class="user" title="Wayne">Wayne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer129948283">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18153361394152379747">This novel was at times gripping, often thought provoking, and frequently fascinating in its stark portrayal of the future that was both alluring and frightening. The way it was structured was interesting in that it was divided into stories, but each story illuminated different parts of a larger picture, and had characters which overlapped from time to time, influencing the outcome and adding a nice texture to the novel. Unfortunately, some of the stories were less than thrilling, let alone fast</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18153361394152379747">This novel was at times gripping, often thought provoking, and frequently fascinating in its stark portrayal of the future that was both alluring and frightening. The way it was structured was interesting in that it was divided into stories, but each story illuminated different parts of a larger picture, and had characters which overlapped from time to time, influencing the outcome and adding a nice texture to the novel. Unfortunately, some of the stories were less than thrilling, let alone fast paced enough to hold interest, but to not finish each would cheapen the overall effect of the book. My biggest problem with the novel was the ever present racial aspect, which I felt weighted the book with too much baggage. I felt the same when I read a few of the Left Behind series, where religion, instead of race, was the weight that burdened an otherwise great thriller with overbearing importance. What I really liked and identified with in Futureland was the individual against overwhelming society, authority, and control, both mental and physical. That aspect made the novel for me.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18153361394152379747">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_40606748">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40606748" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/972013-chris" class="left imgcol" title="Chris"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1205103482p2/972013.jpg" alt="Chris" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/40606748?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 31, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/972013-chris" class="user" title="Chris">Chris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84529.Futureland" title="Paperback 9780446610735" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer40606748">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17770322952127764475">I usually shy away from collections of short stories but <em>Futureland</em> may force me to reevaluate my stance. One of the things I liked most about this novel is the way Mosley weaved the different stories together, often with varying central themes, through the use of recurring characters and ideas. The world he paints can be seen as an extension of our present situation if our most pressing issues are left to fester and boil. Mosley wraps race, class, gender, ethics, science, and technology into so</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17770322952127764475">I usually shy away from collections of short stories but <em>Futureland</em> may force me to reevaluate my stance. One of the things I liked most about this novel is the way Mosley weaved the different stories together, often with varying central themes, through the use of recurring characters and ideas. The world he paints can be seen as an extension of our present situation if our most pressing issues are left to fester and boil. Mosley wraps race, class, gender, ethics, science, and technology into some of the most gripping sci-fi stories I've read. The constructs of Common Ground, where the prods (working class) go on unemployment cycles - essentially a global ghetto for the world's poorest, the different issue-based political parties/alliances (word to the Radical Congress and the FemLeague), corporate states, and struggles around access to life-changing technology for all really resonated with me as I could see how our world may end up running parallel to what Mosley creates. He deftly balances the cynical elements of Futureland with ones that are both redemptive and optimistic. I also can't forget about the characters, who are extremely relatable and memorable - from the genius prodigy communicating with "God" to the villainous mad scientist bent on global domination to the gender-norm breaking boxer and the (several) worker revolutionaries. All in all, <em>Futureland</em> is pure win and Mosley needs to bless us with more amazing sci-fi stylings.<br /><br />The only shortcoming may be that in our "post-racial" society some folks may not appreciate Mosley's intricate mix of social critique within the realm of futuristic sci-fi, but for me it works beautifully. To be honest, I enjoy when writers do more than gush about shiny robots or ramble on about cyber warfare devoid of any social implications - they've got to tether their stories to something within our current reality that can be deconstructed and analyzed. In other words, the more philosophical they get on the human condition the better, regardless of floating pc screens, hyperdrive spaceships, and multi-eyed extraterrestrials. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17770322952127764475">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer12553824085288047431">TITLE: FUTURELAND<br />AUTHOR: WALTER MOSLEY<br />AUDIENCE: GENERAL ADULT NONFICTION, DYSTOPIAN<br />REVIEW:<br />Step inside NYC of 2055, the later half of the twenty-first century, but be careful!<br />In this book,. you see how the trends of today -- corporate greed, government lack of oversight, extreme technological advances, and the management of the unemployed, the racial tension, the prison system filled with black men... take all of what is already wrong, and now, turn up the volume to rabid extremes and make it all</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12553824085288047431">TITLE: FUTURELAND<br />AUTHOR: WALTER MOSLEY<br />AUDIENCE: GENERAL ADULT NONFICTION, DYSTOPIAN<br />REVIEW:<br />Step inside NYC of 2055, the later half of the twenty-first century, but be careful!<br />In this book,. you see how the trends of today -- corporate greed, government lack of oversight, extreme technological advances, and the management of the unemployed, the racial tension, the prison system filled with black men... take all of what is already wrong, and now, turn up the volume to rabid extremes and make it all worse.<br /><br />In 2055, our TVS are vids, and they are 3-D. Sure, you can buy that, right? But now a new model has subsumed all employment. Employment records control where you are permitted to live or work. Laws to protect workers have gone to rabid extremes. There is no autonomy, only obedience to the current assignment. Your whole life experience depends on your worker record. Those who cannot stay employed can live in Common ground -- they get a tube and some beans. But it's the lowest rung of the ladder, the inner circle of hell, where the worst criminals prey on the most defenseless. The entire world of an average person is frankly, hopeless and spent in fear of losing what little they have.<br /><br />Stretched so far, will the reality SNAP? <br />Will these rabid extremes come to a flash point? <br />Will the deep thinkers make a difference in the world?<br />Can anything restore the sense of humanity's nobility?<br /><br />TAGS: Dystopia, Race warfare, class warfare, late 21st century.<br /><br />PERMALINK: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://martysreads.blogspot.com/2013/06/futureland.html/" target="_blank">http://martysreads.blogspot.com/2013/...</a> <br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_667435030">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/667435030" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1363610-kelly" class="left imgcol" title="Kelly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1348547139p2/1363610.jpg" alt="Kelly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/667435030?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 24, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1363610-kelly" class="user" title="Kelly">Kelly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17938131-futureland" title="ebook 9781480422087" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1363610-kelly?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1363610-kelly?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1363610-kelly?shelf=dystopian" class="actionLinkLite">dystopian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer667435030">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8407687297769947659">Original review posted <a rel="nofollow" href="http://populuxe.ca/blog/?p=363">here</a>.<br /><br />This is the perfect book for people who are either scared of, or say they “don’t like” sci-fi. Futureland is an excellent example of how sci-fi can be used to highlight our current world culture, and then cast doubt on the direction we may all be taking. It’s a little bit dystopian, without being fatalist and negative. It’s a little bit cyberpunk, and moments make me think of burning questions that might be brought up by the likes of William Gibson.<br /><br />Heavily philosophic</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8407687297769947659">Original review posted <a rel="nofollow" href="http://populuxe.ca/blog/?p=363">here</a>.<br /><br />This is the perfect book for people who are either scared of, or say they “don’t like” sci-fi. Futureland is an excellent example of how sci-fi can be used to highlight our current world culture, and then cast doubt on the direction we may all be taking. It’s a little bit dystopian, without being fatalist and negative. It’s a little bit cyberpunk, and moments make me think of burning questions that might be brought up by the likes of William Gibson.<br /><br />Heavily philosophical, but not overbearingly so, Mosley doesn’t preach, nor bash you over the head with his complex world and the social ramifications therein. He deals with issues of race, sex (both the physical act as well as physiologically), ethics of bio-medical engineering, class, politics, privacy issues, welfare, reliance on technology, and the justice system. He also deals with the idea of the soul, religion, and faith. It’s a huge undertaking to examine these issues in the span of 300-odd pages, but Mosley does it with class and effectiveness.<br /><br />To call this a short story book is a bit of a misnomer – while it is a collection of short stories, they are all stories set in the same world that are interconnected. Going into the book, I thought these stories were all disparate entities in and of themselves, but in actuality they are all images from a much larger world, and they are all linked together by not a single character, but many. Story A will feature a character, Story B will make a remark of that character and introduce a new one, who in turn in Story C has influenced the world in some way, etc. It’s a wonderful structure that meshes all these stories in perfectly.<br /><br />The world that Mosley creates is constantly in transition, which leads to the credibility of the future that he has created. While I read this book, I feel as thought history is being made now, not in some distant past as is frequently the case with science fiction.<br /><br />Like all short story books there are stories I connected with over others. The first story I barely got into, but that was until I understood how it was an instigator for how the rest of the world would be shaped over the next 30 (maybe 40?) or so years. The timing was a bit hazy. I think what pushed me out the most of this story was the heavy dialect the author opted to write this first story in (which, thankfully, wasn’t throughout the whole book). I’m just generally not a fan of when an author does that, as I find it takes me out of a story if I have to work too hard to figure out just what in the hell the characters are saying.<br /><br />Mosley also plays a little fast and loose with the science part of the sci-fi, and while it’s not meant to be hard science, being of that genre it is inherently present in the book, despite the focus on societal aspects of the world. I won’t say too much as not to spoil it for anybody picking this one up to read (which you really should), but my belief was easily suspended for all science except for the final climactic science-y bit. It was a bit thrown together, and far too much of a leap for me to make. While that was a bit of a let-down, it was still an incredibly enjoyable read.<br /><br />All in all, it was a pleasure to read this book, and I loved the organic nature of Mosley’s story telling, and the way the book was structured as a whole. This is one I would definitely recommend to those looking for some serious meat in their science fiction.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8407687297769947659">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/176348261?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 13, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4044700-angela" class="user" title="Angela">Angela</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4044700-angela?shelf=bookcrossed-or-otherwise-set-free" class="actionLinkLite">bookcrossed-or-otherwise-set-free</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer176348261">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4218747880469816412">Futureland <em>is bestselling mystery author Walter Mosley's first science fiction book since </em>Blue Light<em>, a New York Times Notable Book of the Year. </em>Futureland<em>'s nine linked stories will provide an accessible and intelligent introduction to written science fiction for mystery or mainstream fiction fans who do not normally read the genre.<br /><br />Experienced science fiction readers, however, may be less than satisfied with </em>Futureland<em>. Reading it, you might decide Mr. Mosley grew up reading SF, respects the ge</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4218747880469816412">Futureland <em>is bestselling mystery author Walter Mosley's first science fiction book since </em>Blue Light<em>, a New York Times Notable Book of the Year. </em>Futureland<em>'s nine linked stories will provide an accessible and intelligent introduction to written science fiction for mystery or mainstream fiction fans who do not normally read the genre.<br /><br />Experienced science fiction readers, however, may be less than satisfied with </em>Futureland<em>. Reading it, you might decide Mr. Mosley grew up reading SF, respects the genre, and still watches SF movies, but has read little SF written during or after the New Wave of the 1960s. However, something more may be going on here than a genre newcomer making beginning-SF-writer mistakes. Mr. Mosley may be deliberately, and craftily, creating SF accessible to his large non-SF readership and to others who are strangers to this genre.<br /><br />Some have labeled </em>Futureland<em> cyberpunk, and it does present a dark, infotech-saturated, corporation-controlled future; but it is in fact an inversion of cyberpunk. Instead of that subgenre's cliche of cool, cutting-edge, street-smart, but not very believable outlaws who out-hack and outwit powerful multinational corporations, this Dante-esque collection presents outlaws and outcasts who may be street-wise, but who have little chance of overcoming the corporations and governments that control, and sometimes take, their lives. Like shockingly few other SF works, Futureland directly examines the lives of the working and the nonworking classes, the poor and the marginalized, the criminal and the criminalized. In other words, </em>Futureland<em> is set in a world quite alien to many veteran SF readers, and is therefore a book they should try.</em> --Cynthia Ward (Amazon review)</span>
